			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Nature</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Nature_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Nature (disambiguation)">Nature (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">"Natural" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Natural_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Natural (disambiguation)">Natural (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Hopetoun_falls.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Hopetoun_falls.jpg/300px-Hopetoun_falls.jpg" width="300" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Hopetoun_falls.jpg/450px-Hopetoun_falls.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Hopetoun_falls.jpg/600px-Hopetoun_falls.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Hopetoun_Falls" title="Hopetoun Falls">Hopetoun Falls</a>, Australia</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Galunggung.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Galunggung.jpg/300px-Galunggung.jpg" width="300" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Galunggung.jpg/450px-Galunggung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Galunggung.jpg/600px-Galunggung.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="530" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">Lightning</a> strikes during the eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Galunggung" title="Galunggung">Galunggung</a> <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcano</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>, in 1982</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Nature</b>, in the broadest sense, is the natural, physical, or material world or universe. "Nature" can refer to the <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomena</a> of the physical world, and also to life in general. The study of nature is a large part of science. Although humans are part of nature, human activity is often understood as a separate category from other natural phenomena.</p>
<p>The word <i>nature</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>natura</i>, or "essential qualities, innate disposition", and in ancient times, literally meant "birth".<sup id="cite_ref-etymonline-nature_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-nature-1">[1]</a></sup> <i>Natura</i> is a Latin translation of the Greek word <i><a href="/wiki/Physis" title="Physis">physis</a></i> (φύσις), which originally related to the intrinsic characteristics that plants, animals, and other features of the world develop of their own accord.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The concept of nature as a whole, the physical <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, is one of several expansions of the original notion; it began with certain core applications of the word φύσις by <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratic</a> philosophers, and has steadily gained currency ever since. This usage continued during the advent of modern <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> in the last several centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the various uses of the word today, "nature" often refers to geology and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a>. Nature can refer to the general realm of living plants and animals, and in some cases to the processes associated with inanimate objects–the way that particular types of things exist and change of their own accord, such as the weather and geology of the Earth. It is often taken to mean the "<a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural environment</a>" or <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a>–wild animals, rocks, forest, and in general those things that have not been substantially altered by human intervention, or which persist despite human intervention. For example, manufactured objects and human interaction generally are not considered part of nature, unless qualified as, for example, "human nature" or "the whole of nature". This more traditional concept of natural things which can still be found today implies a distinction between the natural and the artificial, with the artificial being understood as that which has been brought into being by a human <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> or a human <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>. Depending on the particular context, the term "natural" might also be distinguished from the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/unnatural" class="extiw" title="wikt:unnatural">unnatural</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<div id="Legend" class="toccolours" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">billions of years</a>.<br />
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth science</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/200px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/300px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/400px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="3002" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, taken in 1972 by the crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth is the only <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> known to support life, and its natural features are the subject of many fields of scientific research. Within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a>, it is third closest to the sun; it is the largest <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planet</a> and the fifth largest overall. Its most prominent climatic features are its two large polar regions, two relatively narrow <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> zones, and a wide <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial</a> tropical to <a href="/wiki/Subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> region.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a> varies widely with location, from several metres of water per year to less than a millimetre. 71 percent of the Earth's surface is covered by salt-water oceans. The remainder consists of continents and islands, with most of the inhabited land in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>.</p>
<p>Earth has evolved through geological and biological processes that have left traces of the original conditions. The <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">outer surface</a> is divided into several gradually migrating <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plates</a>. The interior remains active, with a thick layer of plastic <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> and an iron-filled core that generates a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. This iron core is composed of a solid inner phase, and a fluid outer phase. Convective motion in the core generates electric currents through dynamo action, and these, in turn, generate the geomagnetic field.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheric</a> conditions have been significantly altered from the original conditions by the presence of life-forms,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> which create an ecological balance that stabilizes the surface conditions. Despite the wide regional variations in climate by <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and other geographic factors, the long-term average global climate is quite stable during interglacial periods,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and variations of a degree or two of average global temperature have historically had major effects on the ecological balance, and on the actual geography of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">Geology</a></div>
<p>Geology is the science and study of the solid and liquid matter that constitutes the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. The field of geology encompasses the study of the composition, <a href="/wiki/Structural_geology" title="Structural geology">structure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_property" title="Physical property">physical properties</a>, dynamics, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Earth">history</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth_materials" title="Earth materials">Earth materials</a>, and the processes by which they are formed, moved, and changed. The field is a major <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" class="mw-redirect" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a>, and is also important for <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mineral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_geology" title="Petroleum geology">hydrocarbon</a> extraction, knowledge about and mitigation of <a href="/wiki/Natural_hazard" title="Natural hazard">natural hazards</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Geotechnical_engineering" title="Geotechnical engineering">Geotechnical engineering</a> fields, and understanding <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">past climates</a> and environments.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_evolution">Geological evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geological evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/200px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" width="200" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/300px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/400px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 2x" data-file-width="775" data-file-height="429" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three types of geological <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Plate tectonic">plate tectonic</a> boundaries.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The geology of an area evolves through time as rock units are deposited and inserted and deformational processes change their shapes and locations.</p>
<p>Rock units are first emplaced either by <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposition</a> onto the surface or intrude into the <a href="/wiki/Country_rock_(geology)" title="Country rock (geology)">overlying rock</a>. Deposition can occur when <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> settle onto the surface of the Earth and later <a href="/wiki/Lithification" title="Lithification">lithify</a> into <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a>, or when as <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_rock" title="Volcanic rock">volcanic material</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a> flows, blanket the surface. Igneous <a href="/wiki/Intrusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Intrusion">intrusions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Batholith" title="Batholith">batholiths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laccolith" title="Laccolith">laccoliths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">dikes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">sills</a>, push upwards into the overlying rock, and crystallize as they intrude.</p>
<p>After the initial sequence of rocks has been deposited, the rock units can be <a href="/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)" title="Deformation (mechanics)">deformed</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphosed</a>. Deformation typically occurs as a result of horizontal shortening, <a href="/wiki/Extension_(geology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Extension (geology)">horizontal extension</a>, or side-to-side (<a href="/wiki/Strike-slip" class="mw-redirect" title="Strike-slip">strike-slip</a>) motion. These structural regimes broadly relate to <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent boundaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundaries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transform_boundary" class="mw-redirect" title="Transform boundary">transform boundaries</a>, respectively, between <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_perspective">Historical perspective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Historical perspective">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Earth">History of the Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pangea_animation_03.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Pangea_animation_03.gif" width="200" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="160" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pangea_animation_03.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An animation showing the movement of the continents from the separation of <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> until the present day.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth is estimated to have formed 4.54&#160;billion years ago from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The moon formed roughly 20&#160;million years later. Initially molten, the outer layer of the Earth cooled, resulting in the solid crust. Outgassing and <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> activity produced the primordial atmosphere. Condensing <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, most or all of which came from <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> delivered by <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">produced the oceans</a> and other water sources.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The highly energetic chemistry is believed to have produced a self-replicating molecule around 4&#160;billion years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hyperia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hyperia.jpg/200px-Hyperia.jpg" width="200" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hyperia.jpg/300px-Hyperia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hyperia.jpg/400px-Hyperia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1673" data-file-height="1576" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hyperia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">Plankton</a> inhabit oceans, seas and lakes, and have existed in various forms for at least 2&#160;billion years.<sup id="cite_ref-Margulis1995_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margulis1995-14">[14]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Continents formed, then broke up and reformed as the surface of Earth reshaped over hundreds of millions of years, occasionally combining to make a <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a>. Roughly 750&#160;million years ago, the earliest known supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, began to break apart. The continents later recombined to form <a href="/wiki/Pannotia" title="Pannotia">Pannotia</a> which broke apart about 540&#160;million years ago, then finally <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, which broke apart about 180&#160;million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neoproterozoic" title="Neoproterozoic">Neoproterozoic</a> era covered much of the Earth in <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and ice sheets. This hypothesis has been termed the "<a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a>", and it is of particular interest as it precedes the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a> in which multicellular life forms began to proliferate about 530–540&#160;million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the Cambrian explosion there have been five distinctly identifiable <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinctions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The last mass extinction occurred some 66 million years ago, when a meteorite collision probably triggered the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">non-avian</a> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and other large reptiles, but spared small animals such as <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>. Over the past 66&#160;million years, mammalian life diversified.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several million years ago, a species of small African <a href="/wiki/Ape" title="Ape">ape</a> gained the ability to stand upright.<sup id="cite_ref-Margulis1995_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Margulis1995-14">[14]</a></sup> The subsequent advent of human life, and the development of agriculture and further <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> allowed humans to affect the Earth more rapidly than any previous life form, affecting both the nature and quantity of other organisms as well as global climate. By comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" title="Great Oxygenation Event">Great Oxygenation Event</a>, produced by the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Siderian" title="Siderian">Siderian</a> period, required about 300&#160;million years to culminate.</p>
<p>The present era is classified as part of a mass <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction event</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a> event, the fastest ever to have occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Some, such as <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, predict that human destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> could cause the extinction of one-half of all species in the next 100&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The extent of the current extinction event is still being researched, debated and calculated by biologists.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:right;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere.2C_climate.2C_and_weather">Atmosphere, climate, and weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Atmosphere, climate, and weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/250px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/375px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg/500px-Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3027" data-file-height="2010" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Top_of_Atmosphere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Blue light is scattered more</a> than other wavelengths by the gases in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, giving the Earth a blue <a href="/wiki/Halo_(optical_phenomenon)" title="Halo (optical phenomenon)">halo</a> when seen from space</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Atmosphere of Earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">Climate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">Weather</a></div>
<p>The Earth's atmosphere is a key factor in sustaining the <a href="/wiki/Nature#Ecosystems" title="Nature">ecosystem</a>. The thin layer of gases that envelops the Earth is held in place by gravity. Air is mostly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, with much smaller amounts of carbon dioxide, argon, etc. The atmospheric pressure declines steadily with altitude. The <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> plays an important role in depleting the amount of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> (UV) radiation that reaches the surface. As <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> is readily damaged by UV light, this serves to protect life at the surface. The atmosphere also retains heat during the night, thereby reducing the daily temperature extremes.</p>
<p>Terrestrial weather occurs almost exclusively in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">lower part of the atmosphere</a>, and serves as a convective system for redistributing heat. <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Ocean currents</a> are another important factor in determining climate, particularly the major underwater <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> which distributes heat energy from the equatorial oceans to the polar regions. These currents help to moderate the differences in temperature between winter and summer in the temperate zones. Also, without the redistributions of heat energy by the ocean currents and atmosphere, the tropics would be much hotter, and the <a href="/wiki/Polar_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar region">polar regions</a> much colder.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif/200px-Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif" width="200" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif/300px-Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif 2x" data-file-width="317" data-file-height="239" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">Lightning</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Weather can have both beneficial and harmful effects. Extremes in weather, such as <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hurricane" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricane">hurricanes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a>, can expend large amounts of energy along their paths, and produce devastation. Surface vegetation has evolved a dependence on the seasonal variation of the weather, and sudden changes lasting only a few years can have a dramatic effect, both on the vegetation and on the animals which depend on its growth for their food.</p>
<p>Climate is a measure of the long-term trends in the weather. Various factors are known to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">influence the climate</a>, including ocean currents, surface <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, variations in the solar luminosity, and changes to the Earth's orbit. Based on historical records, the Earth is known to have undergone drastic climate changes in the past, including <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dszpics1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/200px-Dszpics1.jpg" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/300px-Dszpics1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/400px-Dszpics1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="679" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dszpics1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tornado in central <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The climate of a region depends on a number of factors, especially <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. A latitudinal band of the surface with similar climatic attributes forms a climate region. There are a number of such regions, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climate</a> at the equator to the <a href="/wiki/Polar_climate" title="Polar climate">polar climate</a> in the northern and southern extremes. Weather is also influenced by the seasons, which result from the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis of rotation">axis</a> being <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">tilted</a> relative to its <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">orbital plane</a>. Thus, at any given time during the summer or winter, one part of the Earth is more directly exposed to the rays of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>. This exposure alternates as the Earth revolves in its orbit. At any given time, regardless of season, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_hemisphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern hemisphere">northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_hemisphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern hemisphere">southern</a> hemispheres experience opposite seasons.</p>
<p>Weather is a <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaotic system</a> that is readily modified by small changes to the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a>, so accurate <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">weather forecasting</a> is limited to only a few days.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Overall, two things are happening worldwide: (1) temperature is increasing on the average; and (2) regional climates have been undergoing noticeable changes.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_on_Earth">Water on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Water on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg/300px-44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg" width="300" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg/450px-44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg/600px-44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="12614" data-file-height="4606" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:44_-_Iguazu_-_D%C3%A9cembre_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Iguazu_Falls" title="Iguazu Falls">Iguazu Falls</a> on the border between <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">Water</a></div>
<p><b>Water</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substance</a> that is composed of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and is vital for all known forms of life.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In typical usage, <i>water</i> refers only to its liquid form or <a href="/wiki/States_of_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="States of matter">state</a>, but the substance also has a solid state, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> state, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>. Water covers 71% of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> On Earth, it is found mostly in oceans and other large water bodies, with 1.6% of water below ground in <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> and 0.001% in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">air</a> as <a href="/wiki/Vapor" title="Vapor">vapor</a>, clouds, and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Oceans hold 97% of surface water, <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, and polar <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a> 2.4%, and other land surface water such as rivers, lakes, and ponds 0.6%. Additionally, a minute amount of the Earth's water is contained within biological bodies and manufactured products.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceans">Oceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Oceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg/220px-Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg/330px-Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg/440px-Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ocean_from_Leblon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A view of the Atlantic Ocean from <a href="/wiki/Leblon" title="Leblon">Leblon</a>, Rio de Janeiro.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">Ocean</a></div>
<p>An ocean is a major body of <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">saline water</a>, and a principal component of the hydrosphere. Approximately 71% of the Earth's surface (an area of some 361 million square kilometers) is covered by ocean, a <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">continuous body of water</a> that is customarily divided into several principal oceans and smaller seas. More than half of this area is over 3,000 meters (9,800 feet) deep. Average oceanic <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> is around 35 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_thousand" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per thousand">parts per thousand</a> (ppt) (3.5%), and nearly all seawater has a salinity in the range of 30 to 38 ppt. Though generally recognized as several 'separate' oceans, these waters comprise one global, interconnected body of salt water often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a> or global ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UNAoO_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNAoO-31">[31]</a></sup> This concept of a global ocean as a continuous body of water with relatively free interchange among its parts is of fundamental importance to <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The major oceanic divisions are defined in part by the <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>, and other criteria: these divisions are (in descending order of size) the Pacific Ocean, the Atlantic Ocean, the Indian Ocean, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>, and the Arctic Ocean. Smaller regions of the oceans are called seas, <a href="/wiki/Headlands_and_bays" title="Headlands and bays">gulfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay" title="Bay">bays</a> and other names. There are also <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lakes</a>, which are smaller bodies of landlocked saltwater that are not interconnected with the World Ocean. Two notable examples of salt lakes are the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Salt_Lake" title="Great Salt Lake">Great Salt Lake</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lakes">Lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/220px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/330px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/440px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lake_Mapourika" title="Lake Mapourika">Lake Mapourika</a>, New Zealand</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">Lake</a></div>
<p>A lake (from Latin <i>lacus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Terrain_feature" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrain feature">terrain feature</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Physical_feature" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical feature">physical feature</a>), a body of liquid on the surface of a world that is localized to the bottom of <a href="/wiki/Depression_(geology)" title="Depression (geology)">basin</a> (another type of landform or terrain feature; that is, it is not global) and moves slowly if it moves at all. On Earth, a body of water is considered a lake when it is inland, not part of the ocean, is larger and deeper than a pond, and is fed by a river.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The only world other than Earth known to harbor lakes is <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, Saturn's largest moon, which has lakes of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, most likely mixed with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. It is not known if Titan's lakes are fed by rivers, though Titan's surface is carved by numerous river beds. Natural lakes on Earth are generally found in mountainous areas, <a href="/wiki/Rift_zone" title="Rift zone">rift zones</a>, and areas with ongoing or recent <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciation</a>. Other lakes are found in <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basins</a> or along the courses of mature rivers. In some parts of the world, there are many lakes because of chaotic drainage patterns left over from the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a>. All lakes are temporary over geologic time scales, as they will slowly fill in with sediments or spill out of the basin containing them.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ponds">Ponds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ponds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg/220px-Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg/330px-Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg/440px-Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mill_Pond_Sunset.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Westborough Reservoir (Mill Pond) in <a href="/wiki/Westborough,_Massachusetts" title="Westborough, Massachusetts">Westborough, Massachusetts</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">Pond</a></div>
<p>A <b>pond</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">body</a> of <a href="/wiki/Water_stagnation" title="Water stagnation">standing water</a>, either natural or man-made, that is usually smaller than a lake. A wide variety of man-made bodies of water are classified as ponds, including <a href="/wiki/Water_garden" title="Water garden">water gardens</a> designed for aesthetic ornamentation, <a href="/wiki/Fish_pond" title="Fish pond">fish ponds</a> designed for commercial fish breeding, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_pond" title="Solar pond">solar ponds</a> designed to store thermal energy. Ponds and lakes are distinguished from streams via <a href="/wiki/Current_(stream)" title="Current (stream)">current</a> speed. While currents in streams are easily observed, ponds and lakes possess thermally driven micro-currents and moderate wind driven currents. These features distinguish a pond from many other aquatic terrain features, such as <a href="/wiki/Stream_pool" title="Stream pool">stream pools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tide_pool" title="Tide pool">tide pools</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivers">Rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/220px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/330px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/440px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1718" data-file-height="1331" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> river in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>'s capital city</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">River</a></div>
<p>A river is a natural <a href="/wiki/Watercourse" title="Watercourse">watercourse</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> usually <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>, flowing toward an ocean, a lake, a sea or another river. In a few cases, a river simply flows into the ground or dries up completely before reaching another body of water. Small rivers may also be called by several other names, including stream, creek, brook, rivulet, and rill; there is no general rule that defines what can be called a river. Many names for small rivers are specific to geographic location; one example is <i>Burn</i> in Scotland and North-east England. Sometimes a river is said to be larger than a creek, but this is not always the case, due to vagueness in the language.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A river is part of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrological_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrological cycle">hydrological cycle</a>. Water within a river is generally collected from <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> through <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> recharge, <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrology)" title="Spring (hydrology)">springs</a>, and the release of stored water in natural ice and snowpacks (i.e., from <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Streams">Streams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Streams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawaii_Creek.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Hawaii_Creek.jpg/220px-Hawaii_Creek.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Hawaii_Creek.jpg/330px-Hawaii_Creek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Hawaii_Creek.jpg/440px-Hawaii_Creek.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawaii_Creek.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rocky stream in Hawaii</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">Stream</a></div>
<p>A stream is a flowing body of water with a <a href="/wiki/Current_(stream)" title="Current (stream)">current</a>, confined within a <a href="/wiki/Stream_bed" title="Stream bed">bed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stream_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Stream bank">stream banks</a>. In the United States, a stream is classified as a watercourse less than 60 feet (18 metres) wide. Streams are important as conduits in the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, instruments in <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_recharge" title="Groundwater recharge">groundwater recharge</a>, and they serve as corridors for fish and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a> migration. The biological <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> in the immediate vicinity of a stream is called a <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian zone</a>. Given the status of the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a>, streams play an important <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_corridor" title="Wildlife corridor">corridor</a> role in connecting <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">fragmented habitats</a> and thus in conserving <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. The study of streams and waterways in general involves many branches of inter-disciplinary natural science and engineering, including <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geomorphology" title="Geomorphology">fluvial geomorphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecology" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquatic ecology">aquatic ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">fish biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riparian" class="mw-redirect" title="Riparian">riparian ecology</a>, and others.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystems">Ecosystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ecosystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_loch_lomond.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/200px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/300px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/View_of_loch_lomond.JPG/400px-View_of_loch_lomond.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_loch_lomond.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Loch_Lomond" title="Loch Lomond">Loch Lomond</a> in Scotland forms a relatively isolated ecosystem. The fish community of this lake has remained unchanged over a very long period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aravalli.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aravalli.jpg/200px-Aravalli.jpg" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aravalli.jpg/300px-Aravalli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aravalli.jpg/400px-Aravalli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2888" data-file-height="1926" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aravalli.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lush green Aravalli Mountain Range in the Desert country-<a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, India. A wonder how such greenery can exist in hot Rajasthan, a place well known for its <a href="/wiki/Thar_Desert" title="Thar Desert">Thar Desert</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg/220px-Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg/330px-Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg/440px-Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chicago_Downtown_Aerial_View.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An aerial view of a <a href="/wiki/Human_ecosystem" title="Human ecosystem">human ecosystem</a>. Pictured is the city of Chicago</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">Ecology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">Ecosystem</a></div>
<p>Ecosystems are composed of a variety of <a href="/wiki/Abiotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Abiotic">abiotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biotic_component" title="Biotic component">biotic</a> components that function in an interrelated way.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The structure and composition is determined by various environmental factors that are interrelated. Variations of these factors will initiate dynamic modifications to the ecosystem. Some of the more important components are: <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, atmosphere, radiation from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, water, and living organisms.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PenasBlancas,_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve,_Jinotega_Department,_Nicaragua.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg/200px-PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg/300px-PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg/400px-PenasBlancas%2C_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve%2C_Jinotega_Department%2C_Nicaragua.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PenasBlancas,_part_of_the_Bosawas_Reserve,_Jinotega_Department,_Nicaragua.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Peñas Blancas, part of the <a href="/wiki/Bosaw%C3%A1s_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Bosawás Biosphere Reserve">Bosawás Biosphere Reserve</a>. Located northeast of the city of <a href="/wiki/Jinotega" title="Jinotega">Jinotega</a> in Northeastern Nicaragua.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Central to the ecosystem concept is the idea that <a href="/wiki/Living_organism" class="mw-redirect" title="Living organism">living organisms</a> interact with every other element in their local <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>. Eugene Odum, a founder of ecology, stated: "Any unit that includes all of the organisms (ie: the "community") in a given area interacting with the physical environment so that a flow of energy leads to clearly defined trophic structure, biotic diversity, and material cycles (i.e.: exchange of materials between living and nonliving parts) within the system is an ecosystem."<sup id="cite_ref-Odum1971_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Odum1971-39">[39]</a></sup> Within the ecosystem, species are connected and dependent upon one another in the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, and exchange energy and <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> between themselves as well as with their environment.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The human ecosystem concept is grounded in the deconstruction of the human/nature <a href="/wiki/Dichotomy" title="Dichotomy">dichotomy</a> and the premise that all species are ecologically integrated with each other, as well as with the abiotic constituents of their <a href="/wiki/Biotope" title="Biotope">biotope</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A smaller unit of size is called a <a href="/wiki/Microecosystem" title="Microecosystem">microecosystem</a>. For example, a microsystem can be a stone and all the life under it. A <i>macroecosystem</i> might involve a whole <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregion</a>, with its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wilderness">Wilderness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Wilderness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biogradska_suma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Biogradska_suma.jpg/220px-Biogradska_suma.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Biogradska_suma.jpg/330px-Biogradska_suma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Biogradska_suma.jpg/440px-Biogradska_suma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="556" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Biogradska_suma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">Old growth</a> <a href="/wiki/European_Beech" class="mw-redirect" title="European Beech">European Beech</a> forest in <a href="/wiki/Biogradska_Gora" title="Biogradska Gora">Biogradska Gora</a> National Park, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">Wilderness</a></div>
<p><b>Wilderness</b> is generally defined as areas that have not been significantly modified by human activity. Wilderness areas can be found in preserves, estates, farms, conservation preserves, ranches, <a href="/wiki/Category:National_forests" title="Category:National forests">national forests</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>, and even in urban areas along rivers, gulches, or otherwise undeveloped areas. Wilderness areas and protected <a href="/wiki/Parks" class="mw-redirect" title="Parks">parks</a> are considered important for the survival of certain <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, ecological studies, <a href="/wiki/Habitat_conservation" title="Habitat conservation">conservation</a>, solitude, and <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a>. Some nature writers believe wilderness areas are vital for the human spirit and creativity,<sup id="cite_ref-Man_p155-157_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Man_p155-157-42">[42]</a></sup> and some Ecologists consider wilderness areas to be an integral part of the Earth's self-sustaining natural <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>). They may also preserve historic <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> traits and that they provide <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> for wild <a href="/wiki/Flora_(plants)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flora (plants)">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna_(animals)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fauna (animals)">fauna</a> that may be difficult to recreate in <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arboretum" title="Arboretum">arboretums</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratories</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
