<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg/170px-Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg" width="170" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg/255px-Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg/340px-Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1830" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Malards_in_Golden_Gate_Park.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Female mallard and ducklings – <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a> is essential for continuing life</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">Life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">Biology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">Biosphere</a></div>
<p>Although there is no universal agreement on the definition of life, scientists generally accept that the biological manifestation of life is characterized by <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a>, and response to <a href="/wiki/Stimulus_(physiology)" title="Stimulus (physiology)">stimuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Life may also be said to be simply the characteristic state of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>.</p>
<p>Properties common to terrestrial organisms (plants, animals, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, and bacteria) are that they are cellular, carbon-and-water-based with complex organization, having a metabolism, a capacity to grow, respond to stimuli, and reproduce. An entity with these properties is generally considered life. However, not every definition of life considers all of these properties to be essential. Human-made <a href="/wiki/Artificial_life" title="Artificial life">analogs of life</a> may also be considered to be life.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> is the part of Earth's outer shell&#160;– including land, surface rocks, water, air and the atmosphere&#160;– within which life occurs, and which <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biotic</a> processes in turn alter or transform. From the broadest <a href="/wiki/Geophysiology" class="mw-redirect" title="Geophysiology">geophysiological</a> point of view, the biosphere is the global ecological system integrating all living beings and their relationships, including their interaction with the elements of the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> (rocks), <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> (water), and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> (air). The entire Earth contains over 75&#160;billion tons (150 <i>trillion</i> pounds or about 6.8×10<sup>13</sup>&#160;kilograms) of <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> (life), which lives within various environments within the biosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over nine-tenths of the total biomass on Earth is plant life, on which animal life depends very heavily for its existence.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> More than 2 million species of plant and animal life have been identified to date,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and estimates of the actual number of existing species range from several million to well over 50&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The number of individual species of life is constantly in some degree of flux, with new species appearing and others ceasing to exist on a continual basis.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The total number of species is in rapid decline.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/275px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" width="275" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/413px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/550px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="964" data-file-height="415" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An area of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> shared between <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a> of South America contain the largest <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diversity</a> of species on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Origin of life">origin of life</a> on Earth is not well understood, but it is known to have occurred at least 3.5&#160;billion years ago,<sup id="cite_ref-Origin1_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-59">[59]</a></sup> during the <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">hadean</a> or <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">archean</a> eons on a <a href="/wiki/Early_Earth" title="Early Earth">primordial Earth</a> that had a substantially different environment than is found at present.<sup id="cite_ref-Line_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Line-60">[60]</a></sup> These life forms possessed the basic traits of self-replication and inheritable traits. Once life had appeared, the process of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> resulted in the development of ever-more diverse life forms.</p>
<p>Species that were unable to adapt to the changing environment and competition from other life forms became extinct. However, the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record retains evidence of many of these older species. Current fossil and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> evidence shows that all existing species can trace a continual ancestry back to the first primitive life forms.<sup id="cite_ref-Line_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Line-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The advent of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> in very basic forms of plant life worldwide allowed the sun's energy to be harvested to create conditions allowing for more complex life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The resultant <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> accumulated in the atmosphere and gave rise to the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>. The incorporation of smaller cells within larger ones resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Endosymbiotic theory">development of yet more complex cells</a> called <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Cells within colonies became increasingly specialized, resulting in true multicellular organisms. With the ozone layer absorbing harmful <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a>, life colonized the surface of Earth.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbes">Microbes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Microbes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yellow_mite_(Tydeidae)_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg/170px-Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg/255px-Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg/340px-Yellow_mite_%28Tydeidae%29_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="2560" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yellow_mite_(Tydeidae)_Lorryia_formosa_2_edit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A microscopic mite <i><a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">Lorryia formosa</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Microbe" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbe">Microbe</a></div>
<p>The first form of life to develop on the Earth were microbes, and they remained the only form of life until about a billion years ago when multi-cellular organisms began to appear.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Microorganisms are single-celled organisms that are generally <a href="/wiki/Microscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Microscopic">microscopic</a>, and smaller than the human eye can see. They include Bacteria, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">Fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protista" class="mw-redirect" title="Protista">Protista</a>.</p>
<p>These life forms are found in almost every location on the Earth where there is liquid water, including in the Earth's interior.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Their reproduction is both rapid and profuse. The combination of a high mutation rate and a <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> ability makes them highly adaptable, and able to survive in new environments, including <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> They form an essential part of the planetary ecosystem. However, some microorganisms are <a href="/wiki/Pathogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathogenic">pathogenic</a> and can post health risk to other organisms.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants_and_animals">Plants and animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Plants and animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png/220px-Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png/330px-Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png/440px-Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png 2x" data-file-width="896" data-file-height="1331" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diversity_of_plants_image_version_5.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A selection of diverse <a href="/wiki/Plant_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant species">plant species</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Animal_diversity.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Animal_diversity.png/220px-Animal_diversity.png" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Animal_diversity.png/330px-Animal_diversity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Animal_diversity.png 2x" data-file-width="354" data-file-height="518" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Animal_diversity.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A selection of diverse <a href="/wiki/Animal_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal species">animal species</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Originally <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> divided all living things between plants, which generally do not move fast enough for humans to notice, and animals. In <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>' system, these became the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">kingdoms</a> <a href="/wiki/Vegetabilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetabilia">Vegetabilia</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Plantae" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantae">Plantae</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">Animalia</a>. Since then, it has become clear that the Plantae as originally defined included several unrelated groups, and the <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> and several groups of <a href="/wiki/Alga" class="mw-redirect" title="Alga">algae</a> were removed to new kingdoms. However, these are still often considered plants in many contexts. Bacterial life is sometimes included in flora,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and some classifications use the term <i>bacterial flora</i> separately from <i>plant flora</i>.</p>
<p>Among the many ways of classifying plants are by regional <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">floras</a>, which, depending on the purpose of study, can also include <i>fossil flora</i>, remnants<br />
of plant life from a previous era. People in many regions and countries take great pride in their individual arrays of characteristic flora, which can vary widely across the globe due to differences in climate and <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a>.</p>
<p>Regional floras commonly are divided into categories such as <i>native flora</i> and <i>agricultural and garden flora</i>, the lastly mentioned of which are intentionally grown and cultivated. Some types of "native flora" actually have been introduced centuries ago by people migrating from one region or continent to another, and become an integral part of the native, or natural flora of the place to which they were introduced. This is an example of how human interaction with nature can blur the boundary of what is considered nature.</p>
<p>Another category of plant has historically been carved out for <i>weeds</i>. Though the term has fallen into disfavor among <a href="/wiki/Botanist" class="mw-redirect" title="Botanist">botanists</a> as a formal way to categorize "useless" plants, the informal use of the word "weeds" to describe those plants that are deemed worthy of elimination is illustrative of the general tendency of people and societies to seek to alter or shape the course of nature. Similarly, animals are often categorized in ways such as <i>domestic</i>, <i>farm animals</i>, <i>wild animals</i>, <i>pests</i>, etc. according to their relationship to human life.</p>
<p>Animals as a category have several characteristics that generally set them apart from other living things. Animals are <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> and usually <a href="/wiki/Multicellular" class="mw-redirect" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> (although see <a href="/wiki/Myxozoa" title="Myxozoa">Myxozoa</a>), which separates them from bacteria, <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, and most <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>. They are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a>, generally digesting food in an internal chamber, which separates them from plants and <a href="/wiki/Alga" class="mw-redirect" title="Alga">algae</a>. They are also distinguished from plants, algae, and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> by lacking <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>.</p>
<p>With a few exceptions, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Sea_sponge" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea sponge">sponges</a> (Phylum Porifera), animals have bodies differentiated into separate <a href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological tissue">tissues</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, which are able to contract and control locomotion, and a <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>, which sends and processes signals. There is also typically an internal <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a> chamber. The eukaryotic cells possessed by all animals are surrounded by a characteristic extracellular matrix composed of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and elastic <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a>. This may be calcified to form structures like <a href="/wiki/Animal_shell" class="mw-redirect" title="Animal shell">shells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spicule_(sponge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spicule (sponge)">spicules</a>, a framework upon which cells can move about and be reorganized during development and maturation, and which supports the complex anatomy required for mobility.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_interrelationship">Human interrelationship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Human interrelationship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div id="Container" class="toccolours searchaux" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div class="annot-nudge" style="text-size:100%; float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div id="Legend" class="toccolours" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years</a>.<br />
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Life_timeline" title="Template:Life timeline">Life timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/220px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/330px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/440px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Despite their natural beauty, the secluded valleys along the <a href="/wiki/Na_Pali_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Na Pali Coast">Na Pali Coast</a> in Hawaii are heavily modified by introduced <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> such as <a href="/wiki/She-oak" class="mw-redirect" title="She-oak">She-oak</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although humans comprise only a minuscule proportion of the total living biomass on Earth, the human effect on nature is disproportionately large. Because of the extent of human influence, the boundaries between what humans regard as nature and "made environments" is not clear cut except at the extremes. Even at the extremes, the amount of natural environment that is free of discernible human influence is diminishing at an increasingly rapid pace.</p>
<p>The development of technology by the human race has allowed the greater exploitation of natural resources and has helped to alleviate some of the risk from natural hazards. In spite of this progress, however, the fate of human <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> remains closely linked to changes in the environment. There exists a highly complex feedback loop between the use of advanced technology and changes to the environment that are only slowly becoming understood.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Man-made threats to the Earth's natural environment include pollution, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, and disasters such as oil spills. Humans have contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> of many plants and animals.</p>
<p>Humans employ nature for both leisure and economic activities. The acquisition of natural resources for industrial use remains a sizable component of the world's economic system.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>Some activities, such as hunting and fishing, are used for both sustenance and leisure, often by different people. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> was first adopted around the <a href="/wiki/9th_millennium_BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="9th millennium BCE">9th millennium BCE</a>. Ranging from food production to energy, nature influences economic wealth.</p>
<p>Although early humans gathered uncultivated plant materials for food and employed the medicinal properties of vegetation for healing,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> most modern human use of plants is through agriculture. The clearance of large tracts of land for crop growth has led to a significant reduction in the amount available of forestation and wetlands, resulting in the loss of habitat for many plant and animal species as well as increased erosion.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aesthetics_and_beauty">Aesthetics and beauty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aesthetics and beauty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg/170px-504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg/255px-504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg/340px-504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:504px-Pinguiculagrandiflora1web.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Pinguicula_grandiflora" title="Pinguicula grandiflora">Pinguicula grandiflora</a></i>, commonly known as a <a href="/wiki/Butterwort" class="mw-redirect" title="Butterwort">Butterwort</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beauty in nature has historically been a prevalent theme in art and books, filling large sections of libraries and bookstores. That nature has been depicted and celebrated by so much art, photography, poetry, and other literature shows the strength with which many people associate nature and beauty. Reasons why this association exists, and what the association consists of, are studied by the branch of philosophy called <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>. Beyond certain basic characteristics that many philosophers agree about to explain what is seen as beautiful, the opinions are virtually endless.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Nature and wildness have been important subjects in various eras of world history. An early tradition of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Landscape art">landscape art</a> began in China during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Dynasty art">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907). The tradition of representing nature <i>as it is</i> became one of the aims of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese painting</a> and was a significant influence in Asian art.</p>
<p>Although natural wonders are celebrated in the <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">Book of Job</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a> portrayals in art became more prevalent in the 1800s, especially in the works of the <a href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Romantic movement">Romantic movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">British</a> artists <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">John Constable</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a> turned their attention to capturing the beauty of the natural world in their paintings. Before that, paintings had been primarily of religious scenes or of human beings. <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>'s poetry described the wonder of the natural world, which had formerly been viewed as a threatening place. Increasingly the valuing of nature became an aspect of Western culture.<sup id="cite_ref-History_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-74">[74]</a></sup> This artistic movement also coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">Transcendentalist movement</a> in the Western world. A common classical idea of beautiful art involves the word <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimesis</a>, the imitation of nature. Also in the realm of ideas about beauty in nature is that the perfect is implied through perfect mathematical <a href="/wiki/Substantial_form" title="Substantial form">forms</a> and more generally by <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">patterns in nature</a>. As David Rothenburg writes, "The beautiful is the root of science and the goal of art, the highest possibility that humanity can ever hope to see".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:281</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Matter_and_energy">Matter and energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Matter and energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/220px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/330px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/440px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first few <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">electron orbitals</a> shown as cross-sections with color-coded probability density</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">Matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">Energy</a></div>
<p>Some fields of science see nature as matter in motion, obeying certain laws of nature which science seeks to understand. For this reason the most fundamental science is generally understood to be "<a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>"&#160;– the name for which is still recognizable as meaning that it is the study of nature.</p>
<p>Matter is commonly defined as the substance of which physical objects are composed. It constitutes the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The visible components of the universe are now believed to compose only 4.9 percent of the total mass. The remainder is believed to consist of 26.8 percent <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> and 68.3 percent <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-planck_overview_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-planck_overview-76">[76]</a></sup> The exact arrangement of these components is still unknown and is under intensive investigation by physicists.</p>
<p>The behavior of matter and energy throughout the observable universe appears to follow well-defined <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a>. These laws have been employed to produce <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> models that successfully explain the structure and the evolution of the universe we can observe. The mathematical expressions of the laws of physics employ a set of twenty <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">physical constants</a><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> that appear to be static across the observable universe.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The values of these constants have been carefully measured, but the reason for their specific values remains a mystery.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_Earth">Beyond Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Beyond Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Planets2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Planets2013.jpg/300px-Planets2013.jpg" width="300" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Planets2013.jpg/450px-Planets2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Planets2013.jpg/600px-Planets2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1047" data-file-height="618" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Planets2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">Planets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> <i>(Sizes to scale, distances and illumination not to scale)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NGC_4414_(NASA-med).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/220px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/330px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/440px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1730" data-file-height="1428" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NGC_4414_(NASA-med).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b><a href="/wiki/NGC_4414" title="NGC 4414">NGC 4414</a></b> is a spiral galaxy in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Coma_Berenices" title="Coma Berenices">Coma Berenices</a> about 56,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> in diameter and approximately 60 million light-years from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">Outer space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">Extraterrestrial life</a></div>
<p>Outer space, also simply called <i>space</i>, refers to the relatively empty regions of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of celestial bodies. <i>Outer</i> space is used to distinguish it from <a href="/wiki/Airspace" title="Airspace">airspace</a> (and terrestrial locations). There is no discrete boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> and space, as the atmosphere gradually attenuates with increasing altitude. Outer space within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_space" class="mw-redirect" title="Interplanetary space">interplanetary space</a>, which passes over into <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar space</a> at what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a>.</p>
<p>Outer space is sparsely filled with several dozen types of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> discovered to date by <a href="/wiki/Rotational_spectroscopy" title="Rotational spectroscopy">microwave spectroscopy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">blackbody radiation</a> left over from the <a href="/wiki/Big_bang" class="mw-redirect" title="Big bang">big bang</a> and the origin of the universe, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, which include <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>. There is also some gas, <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a>, and small <a href="/wiki/Meteor" class="mw-redirect" title="Meteor">meteors</a>. Additionally, there are signs of human life in outer space today, such as material left over from previous manned and unmanned launches which are a potential hazard to spacecraft. Some of this <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">debris</a> re-enters the atmosphere periodically.</p>
<p>Although the Earth is the only body within the solar system known to support life, evidence suggests that in the distant past the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> possessed bodies of liquid water on the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> For a brief period in Mars' history, it may have also been capable of forming life. At present though, most of the water remaining on Mars is frozen. If life exists at all on Mars, it is most likely to be located underground where liquid water can still exist.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conditions on the other terrestrial planets, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, appear to be too harsh to support life as we know it. But it has been conjectured that <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, the fourth-largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, may possess a sub-surface ocean of liquid water and could potentially host life.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astronomers have started to discover extrasolar <a href="/wiki/Earth_analog" title="Earth analog">Earth analogs</a> – planets that lie in the <a href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> of space surrounding a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>, and therefore could possibly host life as we know it.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>

<p><b>Philosophy:</b></p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Mother_Nature" title="Mother Nature">Mother Nature</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature (philosophy)">Nature (philosophy)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">Naturalism</a>: any of several philosophical stances, typically those descended from <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a> that do not distinguish the supernatural from nature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This includes the <i>methodological naturalism</i> of natural science, which makes the <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodological</a> assumption that <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observable</a> events in nature are explained only by natural causes, without assuming either the existence or non-existence of the supernatural.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Balance_of_nature" title="Balance of nature">Balance of nature</a> (biological fallacy): A discredited concept of natural equilibrium in predator–prey dynamics.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-etymonline-nature-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-etymonline-nature_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Harper, Douglas. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.etymonline.com/?term=nature">"nature"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Online_Etymology_Dictionary" title="Online Etymology Dictionary">Online Etymology Dictionary</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2006-09-23</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature&amp;rft.atitle=nature&amp;rft.au=Harper%2C+Douglas&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.etymonline.com%2F%3Fterm%3Dnature&amp;rft.jtitle=Online+Etymology+Dictionary&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A useful though somewhat erratically presented account of the pre-Socratic use of the concept of φύσις may be found in Naddaf, Gerard <i>The Greek Concept of Nature</i>, SUNY Press, 2006. The word φύσις, while first used in connection with a plant in Homer, occurs very early in Greek philosophy, and in several senses. Generally, these senses match rather well the current senses in which the English word <i>nature</i> is used, as confirmed by Guthrie, W.K.C. <i>Presocratic Tradition from Parmenides to Democritus</i> (volume 2 of his <i>History of Greek Philosophy</i>), Cambridge UP, 1965.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first known use of <i>physis</i> was by <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> in reference to the intrinsic qualities of a plant: ὣς ἄρα φωνήσας πόρε φάρμακον ἀργεϊφόντης ἐκ γαίης ἐρύσας, καί μοι <b>φύσιν</b> αὐτοῦ ἔδειξε. (So saying, Argeiphontes [=Hermes] gave me the herb, drawing it from the ground, and showed me its <b>nature</b>.) <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> 10.302-3 (ed. A.T. Murray). (The word is dealt with thoroughly in Liddell and Scott's <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://archimedes.fas.harvard.edu/pollux">Greek Lexicon</a></i>.) For later but still very early Greek uses of the term, see earlier note.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Isaac Newton's <a href="/wiki/Philosophiae_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a> (1687), for example, is translated "Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy", and reflects the then-current use of the words "<a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>", akin to "systematic study of nature"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The etymology of the word "physical" shows its use as a synonym for "natural" in about the mid-15th century: <cite class="citation web">Harper, Douglas. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.etymonline.com/?term=physical">"physical"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Online_Etymology_Dictionary" title="Online Etymology Dictionary">Online Etymology Dictionary</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Britannica Online. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/328083/lake">"Lake (physical feature)"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2008-06-25</span></span>. <q>[a Lake is] any relatively large body of slowly moving or standing water that occupies an inland basin of appreciable size. Definitions that precisely distinguish lakes, ponds, swamps, and even rivers and other bodies of nonoceanic water are not well established. It may be said, however, that rivers and streams are relatively fast moving; marshes and swamps contain relatively large quantities of grasses, trees, or shrubs; and ponds are relatively small in comparison to lakes. Geologically defined, lakes are temporary bodies of water.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature&amp;rft.au=Britannica+Online&amp;rft.btitle=Lake+%28physical+feature%29&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2FEBchecked%2Ftopic%2F328083%2Flake&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Man_p155-157-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Man_p155-157_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Botkin, Daniel B. (2000) <i>No Man's Garden</i>, Island Press, pp. 155–157, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1559634650" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 1559634650</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.calacademy.org/exhibits/xtremelife/what_is_life.php">"Definition of Life"</a>. California Academy of Sciences. 2006<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2007-01-07</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature&amp;rft.btitle=Definition+of+Life&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.calacademy.org%2Fexhibits%2Fxtremelife%2Fwhat_is_life.php&amp;rft.pub=California+Academy+of+Sciences&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The figure "about one-half of one percent" takes into account the following (See, e.g., <cite class="citation book">Leckie, Stephen (1999). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.idrc.ca/en/ev-30610-201-1-DO_TOPIC.html">"How Meat-centred Eating Patterns Affect Food Security and the Environment"</a>. <i>For hunger-proof cities: sustainable urban food systems</i>. Ottawa: International Development Research Centre. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-88936-882-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-88936-882-1">0-88936-882-1</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature&amp;rft.atitle=How+Meat-centred+Eating+Patterns+Affect+Food+Security+and+the+Environment&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephen&amp;rft.aulast=Leckie&amp;rft.btitle=For+hunger-proof+cities%3A+sustainable+urban+food+systems&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.idrc.ca%2Fen%2Fev-30610-201-1-DO_TOPIC.html&amp;rft.isbn=0-88936-882-1&amp;rft.place=Ottawa&amp;rft.pub=International+Development+Research+Centre&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>, which takes global average weight as 60&#160;kg.), the total human biomass is the average weight multiplied by the current human population of approximately 6.5&#160;billion (see, <i>e.g.</i>, <cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.census.gov/ipc/www/world.html">"World Population Information"</a>. U.S. Census Bureau<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 28,</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature&amp;rft.btitle=World+Population+Information&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.census.gov%2Fipc%2Fwww%2Fworld.html&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Census+Bureau&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span>): Assuming 60–70&#160;kg to be the average human mass (approximately 130–150&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a> on the average), an approximation of total global human mass of between 390&#160;billion (390×10<sup>9</sup>) and 455&#160;billion kg (between 845&#160;billion and 975&#160;billion lb, or about 423&#160;million–488&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>). The total biomass of all kinds on earth is estimated to be in excess of 6.8&#160;x&#160;10<sup>13</sup>&#160;kg (75&#160;billion short tons). By these calculations, the portion of total biomass accounted for by humans would be very roughly 0.6%.</span></li>
